Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq on the scenario of a mudarib who uses mudarabah capital to buy a slave-girl, who then gives birth to a child, and the mudarib claims paternity. The passage type is an expansion_probe, as the commentator explains the legal reasoning behind each sub-ruling. The key issue is that the claim of paternity (da'wa) is classified as a report (ikhbar), not a legal creation (insha), so it does not require ownership at the time of the claim for it to be effective. Therefore, if the mudarib later becomes owner of the child (by the child being part of the profit share), the claim is effective and the child becomes free in the mudarib's share. This is the position of Abu Hanifa only; the Shaykhayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) may differ. The wala' (patronage) is shared proportionally between the mudarib and the capital owner. The slave-girl becomes umm walad (mother of a free child) for the mudarib, with compensation to the capital owner based on her value and the capital. The passage also treats variations in values and insolvency of the mudarib, and the marginal note questions how dower can be owed if the relationship is by marriage. The commentary follows the author's order: first the base case, then the legal reasoning distinguishing insha and ikhbar, then the implications for liability, then variations in values, and finally the owner's claim. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
المضارب (al-mudarib): The person who manages the mudarabah capital as an agent-trustee.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The owner of the capital in a mudarabah contract.
الولد (al-walad): The child born to the slave-girl, claimed as his own by the mudarib.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne her master a child, thereby gaining freedom upon his death.
السعاية (al-su'ayah): The earning or working of a slave to pay off a debt or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a mudarib buys a slave-girl with mudarabah capital, she gives birth, and the mudarib claims the child as his own, what is the ruling on the child's freedom according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The entire child is free upon the claim.
B) The child is free only in the mudarib's share of the profit.
C) The child remains a slave.
D) The child is free only if the capital owner agrees.

Key Takeaway
The classification of a legal act as insha (creation) or ikhbar (report) determines the conditions for its effectiveness—a claim of paternity is ikhbar, so it is effective upon later ownership, unlike freeing a slave which requires immediate ownership.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

Rules of Mudarib Subcontracting Mudarabah
Commentary on the chapter of a mudarib subcontracting mudarabah with a second party, covering liability, profit distribution, and the owner's choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability rules when a mudarib subcontracts mudarabah with and without the owner's permission, and apply the principle of owner's choice.

Core Idea
A mudarib who subcontracts without permission is not liable until the second works; after work, the owner chooses whom to hold liable.

This lesson examines the detailed rules from the Hanafi school regarding a mudarib who enters into a second mudarabah with another person (subcontracting). The passage explains the consequences for liability and profit distribution based on whether the owner's permission was obtained, and draws an analogy from common hire to justify the second mudarib's profit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine whether the mudarib subcontracts with or without the owner's permission. The ruling differs based on permission.
Without permission, liability is contingent on the second's work.
2
Trace liability before and after work
Before the second works, no one is liable for loss. After work, the owner can choose to hold either the first or second mudarib liable for the capital.
The owner cannot take profit without guaranteeing capital.
3
Apply the owner's choice
If the owner holds the first liable, the mudarabah between first and second is valid. If the second is held liable, he recovers from the first.
If both contracts are valid, profit is shared as agreed; if invalid, fair wage applies.
4
Analyze profit distribution with permission
When subcontracting with permission, the shares are calculated based on the agreed ratios, analogous to a common hireling hiring another.
The second's work is attributed to the first, so the first is entitled to the profit share he stipulated with the owner.

Chapter: The mudarib subcontracts mudarabah. His saying: (If the mudarib subcontracts without permission, he does not guarantee as long as the second has not worked) meaning profit or not; even if it is lost in his hand before work, there is no guarantee on anyone. Similarly, if the second usurps it, the guarantee is only on the usurper. And if the second consumes the capital or gives it as a gift, the guarantee is on him and not on the first. And when the second works, the owner of capital has the choice: if he wishes, he can hold the first liable for his capital, and if he wishes, he can hold the second liable. And if the owner of capital chooses to take the profit and not guarantee, he does not have that right; so it is in al-Mabsut. Then if he holds the first liable, the mudarabah between him and the second is valid, and the profit is on what they stipulated. And if he holds the second liable, the second recovers from the first what he guaranteed, and the mudarabah between them is valid, and the profit is between them, and it is lawful for the second what he profited, not for the first. And if one of them is invalid or both, then there is no guarantee on either of them, and the worker has a fair wage from the first mudarib, and the first recovers it from the owner of capital, and the loss is on the owner of capital, and the profit is between the first and the owner of capital according to the condition after the second takes his wage if the first mudarabah was valid; otherwise, the first mudarib has a fair wage. And if the second pays mudarabah to a third and the third profits or loses, then if the first said to the second 'act in it according to your opinion', then the owner of capital may hold liable whichever of the three he wishes, and the second and the third recover from the first, and the first does not recover from anyone if the owner of capital holds him liable; otherwise, there is guarantee on the first and guarantee on the second and the third; so it is in al-Muhit. His saying: (If he pays with permission with one-third, and it was said 'what God provides is half between us', then for the owner is half, for the first one-sixth, and for the second one-third) means he subcontracts with the permission of the owner of capital. And he had half by his condition, so half remains, and the mudarib stipulated for the second one-third, so he gets one-sixth, and the profit is lawful for all, because the work of the second is work on behalf of the mudarib, like a common hireling if he hires another for less than what he was hired for. And analogous to what is in the book: if he said 'what is in that of provision is half between us' or said 'take this capital on mudarabah with half'; so it is in al-Hidayah and al-Nihayah. His saying: (And if it was said 'what God most high provides is half between us', then for the second is one-third of it, and the remainder between the first and the owner is half) meaning if the owner of capital said that and the case remains as is, because the stipulated condition is what God provides for the mudarib, and here it is two-thirds, so it is divided between them, and for the second is the remaining third by condition. And analogous is 'what you profit in this from something' or 'what you have from it'.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is the commentary (sharh) on the chapter 'The mudarib subcontracts mudarabah' from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The lesson type is expansion_probe, as the commentator explains the matn and provides rulings with reasoning. The passage covers two main scenarios: (1) the mudarib subcontracts without the owner's permission, and (2) he subcontracts with permission. In the first scenario, the mudarib is not liable for the capital until the second person actually works; before work, any loss is without liability on either. If the second works, the owner can choose to hold either the first mudarib or the second liable for the capital. If the first is held liable, his mudarabah with the second is valid. If the second is held liable, he can recover from the first, and the profit is shared between them. If either or both mudarabah contracts are invalid, there is no liability for loss, but the worker gets a fair wage from the first mudarib, who recovers it from the owner. In cases of sub-subcontracting, the owner can hold any of the three liable. In the second scenario (with permission), the profit distribution is worked out based on the agreed shares. The commentator uses analogies from common hire (ijarah) to justify the legitimacy of the second mudarib's profit. The passage follows the author's order: first the matn phrase, then the explanation, then sub-cases. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
ضارب المضارب (dārib al-muḍārib): The first mudarib who enters into a second mudarabah with another person (subcontracting).
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): The fair market wage for the worker's labor, used when the contract is invalid.
الوضيعة (al-waḍī'ah): Loss or deficit in the mudarabah capital.
الأجير المشترك (al-ajīr al-mushtarak): A common hireling who works for multiple parties, analogized to the second mudarib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mudarib subcontracts the mudarabah capital to another person without the owner's permission, and the capital is lost before the second person performs any work, who is liable for the loss?
A) The first mudarib alone.
B) The second mudarib alone.
C) No one is liable.
D) The owner bears the loss.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a mudarib who subcontracts without permission is only liable after the second works, and the owner then chooses whom to hold liable; with permission, the second's profit is lawful by analogy to a common hireling.

Profit Distribution When First Mudarib Gives Half to Second
Ruling with commentary on profit distribution when first mudarib gives half to second

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling and calculate shares when the first mudarib stipulates a half-share for the second mudarib, and compare it to the one-third stipulation.

Core Idea
In mudarabah, the first mudarib's condition for the second determines profit shares; the remainder is split equally between first mudarib and capital owner.

This lesson examines a specific mudarabah scenario where the first mudarib delegates part of the work to a second mudarib and stipulates a condition about sharing his own profits. The ruling clarifies how the profit is distributed when the condition is 'my profit is half between us'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for drafting valid mudarabah contracts and avoiding disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the ruling: the first mudarib says 'my profit is half between us' and gives half of the profit to the second mudarib. The ruling: the second receives that half, and the remaining half is divided equally between the first mudarib and the capital owner.
The key is that the second's share comes from the first's profit, not from the total.
2
Compare with the previous case (one-third stipulation)
In the earlier case, the first mudarib stipulated one-third for the second. There, the second got 1/3, and the remaining 2/3 were split equally between first and owner. Here, the second gets 1/2, so the remaining 1/2 is split. The only difference is the stipulated fraction.
The commentary explicitly highlights this difference.
3
Apply the underlying principle
The principle is that the condition 'my profit is half between us' attributes the profit to the capital (since 'my profit' refers to the yield of the capital). This makes the entire profit the subject of the condition, leading to the described distribution. The first mudarib's share is his half of the remaining profit after the second's stipulation.
If the phrasing were 'whatever profit Allah provides', the distribution would differ because profit would be attributed to the mudarib's work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو قال له ما ربحت بيننا نصفان ودفع بالنصف فللثاني النصف واستويا فيما بقي ولا فرق بين هذه الصورة وما قبلها إلا من حيث اشتراط المضارب للثاني فإن في الأولى شرط له الثلث فكان ما بقي بينهما، وفي الثانية شرط له النصف فكان النصف الباقي بينهما
He said: (And if he says to him: 'What I profited is half between us' and he gives him half, then for the second is half, and they are equal in what remains. And there is no difference between this form and the one before it except in terms of the first mudarib's condition for the second. In the first, he stipulated one-third for him, so what remained was between them; and in the second, he stipulated half for him, so the remaining half is between them.)
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn and its commentary. The matn states: if the first mudarib tells the second mudarib 'my profit is half between us' and then gives him half of the profit, the second receives that half, and the remaining half is divided equally between the first mudarib and the capital owner. The commentary clarifies that this is similar to a previous case (where the second was given one-third) except that the stipulated share for the second differs. In the earlier case, the second got one-third, so the remaining two-thirds were split between the first and the owner. In this case, the second gets half, so the remaining half is split. The effective cause ('illa) is that the condition 'my profit is half between us' attributes the profit to the capital (since 'my profit' refers to the yield of the capital), so the entire profit is considered; whereas in the alternative phrasing 'whatever profit Allah provides' or 'whatever surplus' attributes it to the mudarib's efforts, affecting the distribution. This ruling is based on the principle that the mudarib's share is a reward for his work, and any stipulation must be clear.

Key Terms
المضارب (al-mudarib): The investment agent who manages the capital.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The capital owner who provides funds for mudarabah.
الربح (al-ribh): Profit generated from the mudarabah venture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The capital owner alone
C) The first mudarib and the capital owner equally
D) The second mudarib

Key Takeaway
The first mudarib's condition for the second determines profit shares; the remainder is always split equally between the first mudarib and the capital owner.

Profit Distribution When Attributed to Divine Provision
Ruling with reasoning on profit-share wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit distribution when the share is phrased as 'whatever Allah provides' or 'whatever surplus', and explain the reasoning (ta'lil) behind the first mudarib receiving nothing.

Core Idea
If profit is attributed to Allah's provision or surplus, the first mudarib gets nothing; the second gets the stipulated share.

In mudarabah partnerships, the wording of the profit share is critical. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq shows a scenario where the second mudarib is told 'whatever Allah provides is his half' or 'whatever surplus arises, half between us'. The ruling has surprising consequences for the first mudarib, who ends up with nothing. Understanding the reasoning (ta'lil) is key to avoiding invalid profit-sharing conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verbal formula and stipulated shares
Read the source: the phrases 'ما رزق الله فلي نصفه' and 'ما كان من فضل فبيننا نصفان' indicate profit is attributed to Allah's provision or to surplus. The first mudarib (rabb al-mal) gives the second mudarib half.
The wording must be precise; vague attribution is problematic.
2
Determine who gets what
The text states: 'فللمالك النصف وللثاني النصف ولا شيء للأول' — the owner (capital provider) gets half, the second (the mudarib who actually works? careful: here 'first' and 'second' refer to the two parties? The passage says: 'فللمالك النصف وللثاني النصف ولا شيء للأول'. The 'مالك' is the owner of capital, the 'ثاني' is the second mudarib (the one who is given the capital?), and the 'أول' might be the first mudarib (the one who originally received the capital?). Actually, context: 'ولو قيل ما رزق الله فلي نصفه أو ما كان من فضل فبيننا نصفان فدفع بالنصف فللمالك النصف وللثاني النصف ولا شيء للأول'. The 'أول' is the one who made the statement (the rabb al-mal?), but the distribution: owner gets half, second gets half, first gets nothing. This implies the 'first' is an intermediate party? Possibly the first mudarib (the one who takes the capital and then gives it to a second mudarib). This is a complex multi-party scenario. But the key point: the one who attributed the profit to Allah's provision gets nothing.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The commentator says 'ظاهر حكماً وتعليلاً' — the ruling and reasoning are apparent. The reasoning is that by attributing the profit to divine provision or to 'surplus' (فضل), the profit is not clearly tied to the capital or the work of the mudarib. Therefore, the first (the one who made the attribution) cannot claim a share; only the capital owner (المالك) and the second (the recipient of the capital) share the profit as stipulated.
The ambiguity voids the entitlement of the one who introduced the condition.
4
Analyze the special case: two-thirds for the second
If the second is stipulated two-thirds instead of half, the first must guarantee a sixth for the second. This means the first compensates the second from his own wealth to make up the difference between what the second actually receives (half? actually the second gets two-thirds of profit? Let's derive: if profit is X, second should get 2/3 X, but if the distribution is still half? The text says 'ضمن الأول للثاني سدساً' — the first guarantees a sixth. This likely arises because the actual distribution only gave half to the second, but the stipulation was two-thirds; the first must make up the shortfall of (2/3 - 1/2 = 1/6) out of his own pocket. This reinforces that the stipulation must be honored, and the one who caused the ambiguity (the first) bears the liability.
Compensation (daman) is a financial obligation.
5
Extract the general principle
In a mudarabah contract, profit shares must be clearly specified based on capital and work. Any attribution of profit to an uncertain source like 'Allah's provision' or 'surplus' may invalidate the share of the party who introduced the condition, leaving him with nothing. This highlights the Hanafi emphasis on clarity (wuduh) in contractual terms.
This principle applies to all profit-sharing arrangements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو قيل ما رزق الله فلي نصفه أو ما كان من فضل فبيننا نصفان فدفع بالنصف فللمالك النصف وللثاني النصف ولا شيء للأول ولو شرط للثاني ثلثيه والمسألة بحالها ضمن الأول للثاني سدساً) ظاهر حكماً وتعليلاً.
He said: (And if it is said: 'What Allah provides is his half' or 'What surplus there is, half between us', and he gives him half, then for the owner is half, for the second is half, and nothing for the first. And if he stipulates two-thirds for the second, the case being the same, the first guarantees a sixth for the second.) It is apparent in ruling and reasoning.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage presents another scenario: when the second mudarib is told 'whatever Allah provides is his half' or 'whatever surplus arises, half between us', and the first gives him half, then the owner gets half, the second gets half, and the first gets nothing. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that by attributing the profit to Allah's provision or to 'surplus', the profit is not attributed to the capital or the mudarib's work in a way that would entitle the first mudarib to a share. Furthermore, if the second is stipulated two-thirds, the first must guarantee a sixth for the second, meaning the first compensates from his own wealth to make up the difference, as the stipulation must be honored. The commentary notes that this ruling is 'clear in terms of ruling and reasoning'.

Key Terms
الفضل (al-fadl): Surplus or excess profit; in this context, it refers to the profit that is not clearly attributed to the capital or work.
الضمان (al-daman): Financial guarantee; here, the first mudarib must compensate the second for the shortfall in the stipulated share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The entire profit

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, profit-sharing conditions that attribute profit to divine provision or undefined surplus cause the party who introduced them to forfeit their share, and if a different share was stipulated, they must compensate the other party.

Stipulating a Profit Share for the Owner's Slave in Mudarabah
Ruling with detailed commentary on validity of stipulating for the owner's slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stipulating a profit share for the capital owner's slave in mudarabah and identify the conditions that affect its validity.

Core Idea
A slave's profit share belongs to his master unless he has debt; stipulating work is incidental, not a condition.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the validity of a mudarabah contract where the capital owner stipulates a share of profit for his slave. The ruling hinges on the slave's legal capacity and whether he has debts. Understanding this scenario clarifies the Hanafi position on agency and profit allocation in partnership contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The capital owner stipulates one-third profit for himself, one-third for his slave, and one-third for the mudarib, on condition that the slave works with the mudarib.
The slave is the capital owner's property and may be permitted to trade.
2
Determine the slave's legal status
The slave must be ma'dhun (permitted to trade). His profit share goes to his master unless he has debts; if in debt, it goes to his creditors.
The slave acts as an agent for his master.
3
Assess the condition of the slave's work
The stipulation that the slave works is incidental; the contract is valid even without it. The profit share still belongs to the master.
This applies only to the capital owner's slave.
4
Distinguish other parties
If the profit share is stipulated for the mudarib's slave, or for a third party (e.g., wife, child, stranger), the condition is void and the profit goes to the capital owner.
Exception: for a mukatab (slave earning freedom), the share belongs to him, not his master.
5
Identify invalidating stipulations
Stipulating that the capital owner work with the mudarib, or that the mudarib work with a sub-mudarib, invalidates the mudarabah. However, if a mukatab contracts with his own master, it is valid.
This preserves the mudarib's independent right to work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع أجنبي قوله : (وإن شرط للمالك ثلثه ولعبده ثلثه على أن يعمل منه ولنفسه ثلثه صح) أي لعبد المالك على أن يعمل مع المضارب واشتراط الثلث للعبد اشتراط لمولاه وكان العبد مأذوناً له فتكون حصته من الربح للمولى إن لم يكن على العبد دين وإلا فهو لغرمائه إن شرط عمله وإلا فهو للمولى. وقوله على أن يعمل معه عادي وليس بقيد بل يصح الشرط ويكون السيده وإن لم يشترط عمله وقيد برب المال لأن عبد المضارب لو شرط له شيء من الربح ولم يشترط يعمله لا يجوز، ويكون ما شرط له لرب المال إن كان على العبد دين وإلا لا يصح سواء شرط عمله أو لا ويكون للمضارب وقيد بكون العاقد المولى لأن المأذون لو عقدها. وشرط عمل مولاه لا يصح إن لم يكن عليه دين وإلا صح. وشمل قوله العبد ما لو شرط للمكاتب بعض الربح فإنه يصح وكذا لو كان مكاتب المضارب لكن بشرط أن يشترط عمله فيهما وكان المشروط للمكاتب له لا لمولاه وإن لم يشترط عمله لا يجوز . وعلى هذا غيره من الأجانب فتصح المضاربة وتكون لرب المال ويبطل الشرط، والولد والمرأة كالأجانب هنا؛ كذا في النهاية. وقيد باشتراط عمل العبد لأن اشتراط عمل رب المال المضارب مفسد لها، مع وكذا اشتراط عمل المضارب مع مضاربه أو عمل رب المال مع الثاني؛ كذا في المحيط : بخلاف المكاتب إذا دفع ماله مضاربة وشرط عمل مولاه معه لا يفسد مطلقاً، فإن عجز قبل العمل ولا دين عليه فسدت ولو دفع المكاتب ماله مضاربة إلى مولاه يصح؛ كذا في المحيط.
With a third party. He said: (And if he stipulates for the owner its third, and for his slave its third, on condition that he works with him, and for himself its third, it is valid.) Meaning for the owner's slave that he works with the mudarib, and stipulating the third for the slave is a stipulation for his owner, and the slave is permitted to trade. So his share of profit is for his master if there is no debt on the slave; otherwise it is for his creditors if he stipulated his work. And if he did not stipulate his work, then it is for the master. And His statement 'on condition that he works with him' is incidental, not a condition; rather the condition is valid and it belongs to the master even if he did not stipulate his work. And it is restricted to the owner's slave because if the mudarib's slave were stipulated something of the profit and he did not stipulate his work, it is not valid. And what is stipulated for him is for the capital owner if the slave has debt; otherwise it is not valid whether he stipulated his work or not, and it belongs to the mudarib. And it is restricted by the party being the owner because if a permitted slave made the contract and stipulated the work of his master, it is not valid unless he has debt; otherwise it is valid. And his statement 'slave' includes if he stipulates some profit for the mukatab, for it is valid. Likewise if it is the mudarib's mukatab, but with the condition that he stipulates his work in both cases, and the stipulated amount is for the mukatab, not his master. If he does not stipulate his work, it is not valid. And on this pattern, other third parties: the mudarabah is valid and the profit is for the capital owner, and the condition is void. The child and wife are like third parties here; this is in al-Nihaya. And it is restricted by stipulating the slave's work because stipulating the work of the capital owner with the mudarib invalidates the mudarabah. Likewise, stipulating the work of the mudarib with his sub-mudarib, or stipulating the work of the capital owner with the second mudarib, invalidates it; this is in al-Muhit. Contrary to the case where the mukatab gives his wealth as mudarabah and stipulates the work of his master with him, it does not invalidate absolutely. If he becomes unable (to pay) before work and has no debt, it becomes void. If the mukatab gives his wealth as mudarabah to his master, it is valid; this is in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of a mudarabah contract where the capital owner stipulates a share of profit for his slave and for himself, and for the mudarib (first). The ruling is that such a stipulation is valid if the slave works with the mudarib. The commentary explains that the slave is considered an agent for his master, so the profit share for the slave ultimately goes to the master unless the slave has debts, in which case it goes to his creditors. The condition that the slave works is not strictly required; the stipulation is valid even without it. However, this applies only to the capital owner's slave; if the mudarib's own slave is given a share without stipulating his work, the condition is invalid and the share goes to the capital owner or the mudarib depending on circumstances. For a mukatab (slave earning his freedom), stipulating a share for him is valid, and the profit goes to him, not his master. Third parties (non-slaves) cannot have profit shares stipulated; if done, the condition is void and the profit goes to the capital owner. The commentary also notes that stipulating that the capital owner himself works with the mudarib invalidates the mudarabah, as does stipulating work for the mudarib's sub-mudarib. However, if a mukatab (who is his own master) contracts with his owner, it is valid.

Key Terms
العبد (al-'abd): A slave owned by the capital owner, permitted to trade.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has a contract of manumission: he works to pay for his freedom.
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave given permission by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The slave
B) The capital owner
C) The mudarib
D) The slave's creditors

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi mudarabah, a profit share stipulated for the capital owner's slave is valid and belongs to the master unless the slave has debt.

Rulings on Stipulations in Mudarabah Profit Distribution
Rulings on profit stipulations and profit-for-work principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid stipulations regarding profit distribution in a mudarabah contract and state the Hanafi ruling for each scenario.

Core Idea
Stipulating profit for debt repayment is valid; for charity or third parties without discretion, it is void; profit is always in exchange for work.

In this passage, we examine several ancillary rulings in mudarabah concerning stipulations on profit distribution. The author clarifies that while some conditions are permissible, others are void because they contradict the fundamental nature of the contract. Understanding these rules ensures that profit is allocated correctly between the parties and avoids invalid conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stipulation type
First, determine what the condition requires: is it for paying a debt, for charity, or left to the mudarib's discretion?
The text covers three categories: debt repayment, charitable purposes, and discretionary allocation.
2
Apply the validity rule
For debt repayment (of mudarib or capital owner), the condition is valid a fortiori. For charitable causes (poor, Hajj, freeing slaves), it is void and profit goes to the capital owner. For discretionary allocation, the mudarib may only choose himself or the owner, not a third party.
The underlying principle: profit is a return for work and capital, not a gift to outsiders.
3
Recognize the profit-for-work principle
The text states that stipulating profit in exchange for the mudarib's work is agreed upon. This means the mudarib's profit share is compensation for his labor, which is essential to the contract.
This principle is supported by the example where the capital owner works alongside the mudarib without a share—still valid, showing profit is tied to work.
4
Handle slave mudarib cases
If the mudarib is a slave, his profit goes to his master unless he has debts, in which case it goes to creditors. This applies similarly to mukatab slaves and those whose testimony is not accepted.
This clarifies that the slave's ownership of profit is nominal; it ultimately belongs to the master or creditors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان الاشتراط للعبد اشتراطاً لمولاه فاشتراط بعض الربح لقضاء دين المضارب أو لقضاء دين رب المال جائز بالأولى ويكون المشروط للمشروط له قضاء دينه؛ كذا النهاية. ولا يجبر على دفعه لغرمائه ولو شرط بعض الربح للمساكين أو للحج أو في الرقاب لم يصح ويكون لرب المال ولو شرط البعض لمن شاء المضارب فإن شاءه المضارب لنفسه أو لرب المال صح الشرط، وإن شاءه لأجنبي لم يصح؛ كذا في المحيط. واشتراط أن يكون ربح في مقابلة عمله اتفاقي لأنه لو شرط عمل رب المال مع المضارب ولم يذكر له شيء من الربح فإنه صحيح، سواء كان على العبد دين أو لا يكون العبد مضارباً في حق المولى، فإن كان العبد مديوناً فحصته من الربح لغرمائه، وإن لم يكن فحصته لمولاه وكذلك مكاتبه ومن لم تقبل شهادته
And if the stipulation is for the slave, it is a stipulation for his master. So stipulating some of the profit to pay the debt of the mudarib or to pay the debt of the capital owner is permissible a fortiori, and the stipulated amount is for the one stipulated for to pay his debt; thus in al-Nihaya. And he is not compelled to pay it to his creditors. And if he stipulates some of the profit for the poor, or for Hajj, or for freeing slaves, it is not valid, and it goes to the capital owner. And if he stipulates some of it for whomever the mudarib wishes, then if the mudarib wishes it for himself or for the capital owner, the condition is valid; if he wishes it for a third party, it is not valid; thus in al-Muhit. And stipulating that the profit is in exchange for his work is agreed upon, because if he stipulates the work of the capital owner with the mudarib and does not mention any share of profit for him, it is valid. Whether the slave has debt or not, the slave is a mudarib with respect to the master. If the slave is in debt, his share of profit is for his creditors; if not, his share is for his master. Likewise his mukatab and one whose testimony is not accepted.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage covers several ancillary rulings in mudarabah. First, if the profit share is stipulated to pay off a debt of the mudarib or the capital owner, it is valid and the money is used for that purpose. However, the mudarib is not forced to hand it over to his creditors; he may use it for the debt himself. Second, stipulating profit for charitable causes like the poor, Hajj, or freeing slaves is void, and the profit goes to the capital owner. Third, if the profit is left to the mudarib's discretion, he may allocate it to himself or the owner, but not to a third party. Finally, the principle that profit is in exchange for work (al-ribh fi muqabil al-'amal) is agreed upon. This means that the mudarib is entitled to profit as a reward for his labor. The passage also reiterates that the slave's profit goes to his master or creditors as the case may be.

Key Terms
قضاء الدين (qada' al-dayn): Payment of debt; stipulating profit to settle debts of mudarib or owner.
المساكين (al-masakin): The poor; stipulating profit for them is void.
الحج (al-hajj): Pilgrimage; stipulating profit for Hajj expenses is void.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a mudarabah contract stipulates that some profit will be used to free slaves, what is the ruling?
A) The profit is given to free slaves
B) The profit goes to the capital owner
C) The profit is divided between mudarib and owner
D) The contract becomes invalid

Key Takeaway
Stipulations in mudarabah must be consistent with the profit-for-work principle; debt repayment is valid, but charitable or third-party allocations are void and revert to the capital owner.

Invalidation of Mudarabah and Distinction from Agency
Ruling on invalidation of mudarabah with comparative liability and agency distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a mudarabah contract becomes void according to the Hanafi school and distinguish its liability and dismissal rules from those of agency (wakalah).

Core Idea
Mudarabah voids by death or owner's apostasy with enemy joinder; mudarib's apostasy does not void, with distinct liability from agency.

This lesson examines the invalidation of mudarabah and its unique financial consequences compared to agency. The matn states that death of either party voids the contract, as does the capital owner's apostasy if he joins enemy territory. However, the mudarib's own apostasy, according to Abu Hanifa, does not affect the contract's validity. The commentary elaborates on the differences in liability and dismissal between a mudarib and a wakil.

Guided Path
1
Identify voiding events
Locate the two events stated in the matn that cause the mudarabah contract to become void: death of either party and the capital owner's apostasy with joinder to enemy territory.
Death voids because mudarabah is a type of agency.
2
Understand the analogy to death
Analyze why the owner's apostasy with joinder is treated like death: it severs legal capacity and ability to contract.
Simple apostasy without joinder only suspends transactions.
3
Distinguish mudarib's apostasy
Note that according to Abu Hanifa, the mudarib's apostasy does not void the contract, unlike the owner's. Find the explicit statement in the text.
The mudarib remains a trustee; the owner's capital is not affected.
4
Compare liability rules
Examine the difference in financial liability between a mudarib and a wakil when paying the purchase price: the wakil's claim against the principal is limited; the mudarib can claim repeatedly.
The mudarib holds the funds as amanah after payment, not as a debt.
5
Note dismissal and return effects
Observe that a mudarib who has purchased goods is not dismissed upon knowledge, unlike a wakil. Also, if the owner returns from apostasy as a Muslim, the mudarib's contract continues.
The mudarib has a right to complete transactions to protect the capital.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وتبطل بموت أحدهما لكونها وكالة وهي تبطل بالموت قوله : (وبلحوق المالك مرتداً لأنه بمنزلة الموت . وإنما لم يجعل المضارب بمنزلة الوكيل فيما لو دفع إليه الثمن قبل الشراء وهلك في يده بعد الشراء فإن الوكيل يرجع به على الموكل، ثم لو هلك ما أخذه منه ثانياً لا يرجع به مرة أخرى بخلاف المضارب يرجع به على رب المال مرة بعد أخرى إلى أن يصل الثمن إلى البائع لأن شراء الوكيل يوجب الثمن عليه للبائع وله على الموكل، فإذا رجع على الموكل بعد الشراء صار مقتضياً ما استوجبه ديناً عليه وصار مضموناً عليه بالقبض فيهلك من ضمانه. وأما المضارب إذا رجع على رب المال فما يقبضه يكون أمانة، فإذا هلك كان على رب المال فيرجع مرة بعد أخرى، وفيما إذا اشترى بمال المضاربة عروضاً ثم عزل لا ينعزل وإن علم والوكيل ينعزل وسيأتي الفرق بينهما، وفيما إذا عاد رب بعد اللحوق مسلماً فالمضارب على مضاربته بخلاف الوكيل والفرق أن محل التصرف خرج عن ملك الموكل ولم يتعلق به حق الوكيل بخلاف المضارب. قيد بلحوق المالك لأن المالك لو ارتد ولم يلحق فتصرفه موقوف. وأشار إلى أن المضارب لو ارتد فالمضاربة على حالها لو اشترى وباع وربح أو خسر ثم قتل على ردته أو مات أو لحق بدار الحرب فإن التصرف جائز والربح بينهما على ما شرطا والعهدة في جميع تصرفه على رب المال في قول أبي حنيفة .
He said: (And it becomes void by the death of either one, because it is an agency, and it is voided by death. He said: (And by the owner joining the enemy as an apostate, because it is like death. And he did not make the mudarib like the agent in the case where he pays the price before purchase and it perishes in his hand after purchase: the agent claims it back from the principal, then if what he took from him perishes again, he cannot claim it again, unlike the mudarib who claims from the capital owner repeatedly until the price reaches the seller, because the agent's purchase makes the price due from him to the seller and from the principal to him. So when he claims from the principal after purchase, he has collected a debt owed to him, and it becomes a guarantee in his hand, so it perishes from his guarantee. As for the mudarib, when he claims from the capital owner, what he receives is a trust, so if it perishes, it is upon the capital owner, and he can claim repeatedly. And in the case where he buys goods with the mudarabah capital and then is dismissed, he is not dismissed if he knows, whereas the agent is dismissed. And the difference between them will come later. And in the case where the owner returns after joining the enemy as a Muslim, the mudarib remains on his mudarabah, unlike the agent. The difference is that the subject of transaction has left the ownership of the principal and the agent has no right attached to it, unlike the mudarib. It is restricted by the owner joining the enemy because if the owner apostatizes but does not join, his transactions are suspended. And he indicated that if the mudarib apostatizes, the mudarabah remains as it is: if he buys and sells and makes profit or loss, then if he is killed for his apostasy, or dies, or joins the enemy's territory, the transactions are valid and the profit is between them as stipulated, and liability for all his transactions is on the capital owner according to Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage addresses the invalidation of mudarabah. The matn states that the contract becomes void upon the death of either party (mudarib or capital owner) because it is a type of agency (wakalah), which ends with death. It also becomes void if the capital owner becomes an apostate and joins enemy territory (mulhiq), as this is legally like death. However, if the owner apostatizes but does not join the enemy, his transactions are suspended. The commentary then contrasts the mudarib's position with that of a regular agent (wakil) in terms of financial liability when paying the purchase price. If a wakil pays the price before receiving the goods and the money perishes, he can claim from the principal once; if he receives replacement and that perishes, he cannot claim again because it became his guarantee. The mudarib, however, can claim repeatedly from the capital owner because what he receives after payment is an amanah (trust) in his hands, not a guarantee. Additionally, if a mudarib is dismissed after purchasing goods, he is not automatically dismissed upon knowledge, whereas an agent is. If the owner returns as a Muslim after apostasy, the mudarib's contract remains valid. A notable intra-Hanafi point: according to Abu Hanifa, even if the mudarib himself apostatizes, his transactions are valid and the owner bears liability. This is the view of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wikalah): Agency contract; mudarabah is considered a type of agency.
الردة (al-riddah): Apostasy from Islam; affects legal capacity.
اللحوق بدار الحرب (al-luhuq bi-dar al-harb): Joining enemy territory; legally equivalent to death in some rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens to the mudarabah contract if the mudarib apostatizes?
A) It becomes void immediately
B) It remains valid and transactions continue
C) It is suspended until he returns to Islam
D) The owner becomes liable for all losses

Key Takeaway
Mudarabah voids by death or owner's apostasy with enemy joinder, but the mudarib's apostasy does not void the contract according to Abu Hanifa, with distinct liability rules from agency.

When Does Dismissal of a Mudarib Take Effect?
Ruling with commentary on conditions for dismissal to take effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a mudarib is dismissed and identify the required level of knowledge for dismissal to take effect.

Core Idea
A mudarib is dismissed only upon actual knowledge of the dismissal, not upon the owner's mere declaration.

In Hanafi fiqh, mudarabah is a form of agency, so revocation of the mudarib's authority follows the rules of agency. But when exactly does the dismissal become effective? The answer depends on whether the mudarib knows of the dismissal. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the Hanafi definition of knowledge in this context.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling
Identify the central rule: the mudarib is dismissed by the capital owner's dismissal only if he knows of it. Write it in your own words.
The key phrase is 'ينعزل بعزله إن علم'
2
Identify the condition
The dismissal is conditional on the mudarib's knowledge. Without knowledge, the dismissal is not effective and the mudarib's actions remain valid.
The reason given: 'لأنه وكيل' – because he is an agent.
3
Define 'knowledge' in this context
The text defines 'ilm as information received from specific sources. Note the three categories: report of two men (absolute), report of one just man if the mudarib is a fuduli, and report of a discerning child otherwise.
The definition is operational, not theoretical.
4
Distinguish based on the mudarib's status
The evidence required to establish knowledge depends on whether the mudarib is a fuduli (volunteer) or not. A fuduli needs only one just witness; a non-fuduli can be informed by a discerning child.
This distinction reflects Hanafi principles of testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وينعزل بعزله إن علم أي ينعزل المضارب بعزل رب المال إن علم به لأنه وكيل، وإن لم يعلم لا . والمراد بالعلم ما يستفاد من خبر رجلين مطلقاً أو واحد عدل إن كان فضولياً وإلا فخبر مميز
He said: (And he is dismissed by his dismissal if he knows.) That is, the mudarib is dismissed by the capital owner's dismissal if he knows of it, because he is an agent; if he does not know, he is not dismissed. And what is meant by knowledge is what is obtained from the report of two men absolutely, or one just man if the mudarib is a volunteer (fuduli); otherwise, the report of a discerning child.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage explains when a mudarib's dismissal takes effect. Since mudarabah is a form of agency, the mudarib is dismissed when the capital owner revokes his permission. However, the dismissal only takes effect when the mudarib actually knows of it. Until then, his actions are valid. The commentary defines 'knowledge' as receiving information from two reliable male witnesses (generally) or from one just witness if the mudarib is a fuduli (someone who acts without prior authorization). If the mudarib is not a fuduli, then even the report of a discerning child may suffice. The point is that the mudarib must have actual knowledge, not just presumption.

Key Terms
العزل (al-'azl): Dismissal or revocation of authority by the capital owner.
العلم (al-'ilm): Knowledge; defined as receiving reliable information.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): A person who acts without prior authorization; here, if the mudarib is a fuduli, one just witness suffices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the dismissal of a mudarib take effect?
A) Immediately when the owner declares it
B) When the mudarib receives knowledge of it
C) When a witness informs the mudarib
D) Only when the mudarib confirms in writing

Key Takeaway
A mudarib's dismissal takes effect only upon his actual knowledge, which is established by specific evidence depending on his status.

Mudarib's Sale After Dismissal: Profit Right Protects Sale
Ruling with commentary on mudarib's sale after dismissal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mudarib's sale after learning of dismissal and explain why the owner cannot cancel the sale.

Core Idea
The mudarib's right to profit shields the sale from cancellation even after dismissal.

In mudarabah, a mudarib may learn of his dismissal after having purchased goods. This lesson examines the legal effect of selling those goods after becoming aware of dismissal. The Hanafi school holds that the sale is valid and the owner cannot rescind it, but the mudarib may not use the proceeds for further trading. The rationale is the mudarib's vested right to profit in that transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize that the mudarib knows of his dismissal and the capital consists of goods (al-'urud). He sells those goods. What are the legal consequences?
The goods are already purchased, so the mudarib is selling inventory.
2
Extract the Two Rulings
From the text, note: (1) the mudarib does not dispose of the price (ثمن), (2) the owner does not have the right to cancel the sale (فسخ).
These are the two key outcomes.
3
Understand the Talil (Reasoning)
The text states: 'because the mudarib has a right in the profit.' This right gives the mudarib a stake in the transaction that prevents the owner from unilaterally voiding it.
The profit right attaches at the moment of sale, even if later discovered.
4
Recognize the Scope
The ruling is restricted to mudarabah ('restricted by mudarabah'), not general agency. In regular wakalah, dismissal may allow the principal to cancel.
The unique profit-sharing nature of mudarabah justifies this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن علم والمال عروض باعها ثم لا يتصرف في ثمنها ولا يملك المالك فسخها في هذه الحالة) لأن للمضارب حقاً في الربح. قيد بالمضاربة لأن أحد
He said: (And if he knows, and the capital is goods, he sold them, then he does not dispose of their price, and the owner does not have the right to cancel the sale in this case) because the mudarib has a right in the profit. It is restricted by mudarabah because one...
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where the mudarib becomes aware of his dismissal after having purchased goods (inventory) with the mudarabah capital. He sells those goods, but then he may not use the proceeds (the price) for further trading because his authority has ended. At the same time, the capital owner cannot cancel the sale that the mudarib conducted after the dismissal, because the mudarib had a right to the profit in that transaction. The phrase 'restricted by mudarabah' indicates that this rule applies specifically to mudarabah, not to regular agency. The complete ruling would be elaborated on the next page.

Key Terms
العروض (al-'urud): Goods or commodities purchased with mudarabah capital.
الثمن (al-thaman): The sale price received for the goods.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Cancellation or rescission of a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Return them to the owner
B) Use them for further trade
C) Keep them as his own
D) Distribute them as profit

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a mudarib's sale after dismissal is valid due to his profit right, but he may not use the proceeds for further trade.

Mudarib's Post-Separation Authority and Debt Collection
Ruling on mudarib's authority and debt collection after contract end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mudarib's authority after separation from the capital owner and determine when debt collection is obligatory.

Core Idea
After mudaraba ends, the mudarib cannot dispose of goods; debt collection is obligatory only if profit exists.

This passage from Bahir al-Raiq clarifies the mudarib's obligations after the mudaraba contract concludes by separation (iftaq). It addresses two key points: the mudarib's prohibition from handling the sale price of goods, and the conditional duty to collect outstanding debts. Understanding these rules ensures proper winding up of a mudaraba partnership according to Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited act after separation
The text states 'لا يتصرف في ثمنها' – the mudarib cannot dispose of the price (thaman) of goods sold during the mudaraba after separation. Determine what 'dispose' means here (any use or transfer).
The prohibition applies even if the mudarib and owner have physically separated (iftaq).
2
Identify the condition for debt collection obligation
The phrase 'وفي المال ديون وربح أجبر على اقتضاء الديون' indicates that the mudarib is compelled to collect debts only when there is profit (ribh) in the mudaraba capital alongside debts. Recognize profit as the trigger.
If no profit exists, collection is not binding on the mudarib.
3
State the ruling when profit is absent
When there is no profit, the text says 'وإلا لا يلزمه الاقتضاء' – collection is not compulsory. The owner can appoint an agent (يوكّل المالك عليه).
The mudarib is not forced to act as collector without profit.
4
Note the owner's option to appoint an agent
The owner (rabb al-mal) has the right to authorize someone else (e.g., a wakil) to collect the debts, as the mudarib is not obliged.
This preserves the owner's right to recover debts without relying on the mudarib.
5
Identify the broker's duty
The samsar (broker) is compelled to demand payment (التقاضي) from the buyer, as it falls within his work scope.
Unlike the mudarib without profit, the broker's duty is unconditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يتصرف في ثمنها ولو افترقا وفي المال ديون وربح أجبر على اقتضاء الديون وإلا لا يلزمه الاقتضاء ويوكل المالك عليه والسمسار يجبر على التقاضي
He does not dispose of its price even if they separate. And if there are debts in the capital and profit, he is compelled to collect the debts; otherwise, collection is not binding on him. The owner appoints an agent for collection, and the broker is compelled to demand payment.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: when a mudaraba contract ends by separation (iftaq), the mudarib cannot use the sale price of any goods he sold. However, if there are debts owed to the mudaraba and there is profit, the mudarib is compelled to collect those debts. If there is no profit, collection is not binding on him. The owner (rabb al-mal) can appoint an agent to collect debts. The broker (samsar) is compelled to demand payment from the buyer, as it is part of his work.

Key Terms
مُضَارَبَة (mudaraba): A partnership in profit where one party provides capital (rabb al-mal) and the other provides labor (mudarib).
سِمْسَار (samsar): A broker who mediates between buyer and seller for a fee without being hired specifically.
اِفْتِرَاق (iftiraq): Separation; in this context, the physical or legal parting of the mudarib and capital owner, ending the mudaraba contract.
اِقْتِضَاء (iqtidaa): Collection of a debt; demanding and receiving payment.
رِبْح (ribh): Profit generated from the mudaraba venture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the mudarib compelled to collect debts after separation?
A) When there is profit in the mudaraba
B) Always
C) When the owner orders him
D) Only if the mudaraba is profitable

Key Takeaway
The mudarib cannot dispose of the sale price after separation; debt collection is only compulsory when profit exists, otherwise the owner may appoint an agent.

Valid and Invalid Profit Stipulations in Mudaraba
Rulings on stipulating profit for third parties and slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the validity or invalidity of various profit stipulations in mudaraba and apply the ruling to scenarios involving slaves, creditors, and charity.

Core Idea
Stipulating profit for charity is invalid; profit remains with the owner.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a series of rulings on who can be designated to receive profit in a mudaraba contract. The key principle is that stipulations must be for a specific identifiable person or purpose that is legally recognized; otherwise the profit defaults to the owner. Understanding these nuances is crucial for structuring valid mudaraba contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stipulation type
Determine what the condition concerns: debt repayment, charity, a chosen person, or a slave's profit.
Each stipulation has a distinct ruling.
2
Apply the ruling to debt repayment
Stipulating profit to pay a debt of the mudarib or owner is valid; the profit goes to the debtor.
He is not compelled to use it for that debt.
3
Apply the ruling to charity and general purposes
Stipulating for the poor, Hajj, or freeing slaves is invalid; profit belongs to the owner.
The condition is void but contract remains valid.
4
Apply the ruling to mudarib's choice
Stipulating for whomever the mudarib wishes: valid only if he chooses himself or the owner; choosing a stranger invalid.
Mudarib cannot transfer profit to a third party without owner's consent.
5
Apply slave/mukatab rulings
A slave's profit goes to master unless slave has debts; then to creditors. Same for mukatab and those whose testimony is rejected (slaves).
Slave acts as mudarib in relation to his master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان الاشتراط للعبد اشتراطاً لمولاه فاشتراط بعض الربح لقضاء دين المضارب أو لقضاء دين رب المال جائز بالأولى ويكون المشروط للمشروط له قضاء دينه؛ كذا النهاية. ولا يجبر على دفعه لغرمائه ولو شرط بعض الربح للمساكين أو للحج أو في الرقاب لم يصح ويكون لرب المال ولو شرط البعض لمن شاء المضارب فإن شاءه المضارب لنفسه أو لرب المال صح الشرط، وإن شاءه لأجنبي لم يصح؛ كذا في المحيط. واشتراط أن يكون ربح في مقابلة عمله اتفاقي لأنه لو شرط عمل رب المال مع المضارب ولم يذكر له شيء من الربح فإنه صحيح، سواء كان على العبد دين أو لا يكون العبد مضارباً في حق المولى، فإن كان العبد مديوناً فحصته من الربح لغرمائه، وإن لم يكن فحصته لمولاه وكذلك مكاتبه ومن لم تقبل شهادته
If the stipulation is for a slave as a condition for his master, then stipulating some profit for paying the debt of the mudarib or the owner is valid by priority, and the stipulated amount goes to the stipulator to pay his debt. He is not compelled to give it to his creditors. If he stipulates some profit for the poor, for Hajj, or for freeing slaves, it is invalid and belongs to the owner. If he stipulates some for whomever the mudarib wishes, and the mudarib wishes it for himself or for the owner, the condition is valid; if for a stranger, it is invalid. Stipulating a profit in exchange for work is agreed upon because if he stipulates the owner's work with the mudarib and does not mention any profit for him, it is valid. Whether the slave has a debt or not, the slave is a mudarib with respect to his master. If the slave is in debt, his share of profit goes to his creditors; otherwise, to his master. Similarly for his mukatab and one whose testimony is not accepted.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage discusses various conditions (shurut) that can be stipulated in a mudaraba contract regarding the allocation of profit. If the profit is stipulated to pay a debt of the mudarib or the owner, it is valid and the profit goes to the debtor to pay that debt, but he is not forced to actually pay his creditors with that profit. If profit is stipulated for charity (poor, Hajj, freeing slaves), the condition is invalid and the profit remains with the owner. If profit is stipulated for whomever the mudarib chooses, it is valid only if he chooses himself or the owner; choosing a stranger is invalid. Finally, if the mudaraba involves a slave (owned by someone else), the slave's profit belongs to his master unless the slave has debts, in which case profit goes to his creditors. The same ruling applies to a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) and to someone whose testimony is not accepted (meaning a slave).

Key Terms
مُكَاتَب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract with his master to purchase his freedom in installments.
مُضَارِب (mudarib): The party who manages the capital in a mudaraba contract and is entitled to a share of the profit.
رَبُّ الْمَال (rabb al-mal): The investor who provides capital in a mudaraba contract.
شَرْط (shart): A condition stipulated in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mudaraba contract, the investor stipulates that profit be used to pay off the mudarib's existing debt. According to the Hanafi school, this condition is:
A) Valid, and the profit must be given to the creditor
B) Valid, but the mudarib is not forced to pay his debt
C) Invalid, because debt repayment is not a valid purpose
D) Invalid, because the mudarib cannot benefit from the profit

Key Takeaway
Profit stipulations in mudaraba are valid only if they benefit a specific party or serve a legally recognized purpose like debt repayment; indefinite charitable purposes are invalid and profit reverts to the owner.

Termination of Mudaraba by Death or Apostasy and the Mudarib-Wakil Distinction
Ruling that mudaraba terminates with death or apostasy and its effect on recovery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that mudaraba terminates upon death or apostasy (lahaq) of either party, and explain why the mudarib's recovery of funds after purchase is treated as amana rather than debt, distinguishing him from a wakil.

Core Idea
Mudaraba ends with death or apostasy of either party; mudarib's repeated recovery from owner is allowed because he holds funds as amana, not debt.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the termination of mudaraba by death or apostasy. It then contrasts the mudarib with an agent (wakil) in a critical scenario: when the mudarib must pay the seller and the money perishes en route. Understanding this distinction is essential for knowing the liability structure in mudaraba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Termination Triggers
The passage states mudaraba terminates (tabtul) due to death of either party or the owner joining apostates (lahaq bi'l-murtaddin), similar to wakalah termination.
2
Understand the Mudarib vs Wakil Scenario
Both mudarib and wakil purchase goods and pay the price, but money perishes after payment. How do they recover from the owner/principal?
3
Analyze the Difference: Debt vs Amana
The wakil's recovery becomes a debt; if it perishes a second time, he cannot recover again. The mudarib's recovery is amana, so he can recover repeatedly until price reaches seller.
Key: what the mudarib receives is a trust (amana), not a guaranteed debt.
4
Apply the Rule to Other Cases
The passage extends: setting aside goods does not dismiss the mudarib; return of apostate owner still keeps mudaraba; mudarib's own apostasy does not invalidate transactions per Abu Hanifa.
5
Conclude the Legal Effect
The mudarib is not merely an agent; his possession of mudaraba capital and purchased goods is amana, protecting him from liability for loss and allowing repeated recovery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتبطل بموت أحدهما لكونها وكالة وهي تبطل بالموت قوله : (وبلحوق المالك مرتداً لأنه بمنزلة الموت . وإنما لم يجعل المضارب بمنزلة الوكيل فيما لو دفع إليه الثمن قبل الشراء وهلك في يده بعد الشراء فإن الوكيل يرجع به على الموكل، ثم لو هلك ما أخذه منه ثانياً لا يرجع به مرة أخرى بخلاف المضارب يرجع به على رب المال مرة بعد أخرى إلى أن يصل الثمن إلى البائع لأن شراء الوكيل يوجب الثمن عليه للبائع وله على الموكل، فإذا رجع على الموكل بعد الشراء صار مقتضياً ما استوجبه ديناً عليه وصار مضموناً عليه بالقبض فيهلك من ضمانه. وأما المضارب إذا رجع على رب المال فما يقبضه يكون أمانة، فإذا هلك كان على رب المال فيرجع مرة بعد أخرى، وفيما إذا اشترى بمال المضاربة عروضاً ثم عزل لا ينعزل وإن علم والوكيل ينعزل وسيأتي الفرق بينهما، وفيما إذا عاد رب بعد اللحوق مسلماً فالمضارب على مضاربته بخلاف الوكيل والفرق أن محل التصرف خرج عن ملك الموكل ولم يتعلق به حق الوكيل بخلاف المضارب. قيد بلحوق المالك لأن المالك لو ارتد ولم يلحق فتصرفه موقوف. وأشار إلى أن المضارب لو ارتد فالمضاربة على حالها لو اشترى وباع وربح أو خسر ثم قتل على ردته أو مات أو لحق بدار الحرب فإن التصرف جائز والربح بينهما على ما شرطا والعهدة في جميع تصرفه على رب المال في قول أبي حنيفة .
And it becomes void by the death of either one because it is an agency, and agency becomes void by death. And [the text says:] 'And by the owner joining the apostates (lahaq bi'l-murtaddin) because it is like death.' The mudarib is not treated like an agent in that if he has paid the price before purchase and it perishes in his hand after purchase, the agent recovers it from the principal, then if what he took from him perishes again, he cannot recover it again. But the mudarib recovers from the owner again and again until the price reaches the seller. Because the agent's purchase makes the price a liability on him for the seller and he has a claim against the principal. So when he recovers from the principal after purchase, he becomes a claimant of a debt owed to him, and it becomes guaranteed by his possession, so it perishes under his guarantee. As for the mudarib, when he recovers from the owner, what he receives is a trust (amana). So if it perishes, it is on the owner, and he recovers again and again. And if he has bought goods with mudaraba capital and then sets them aside, he is not dismissed even if he knows, whereas the agent is dismissed. And if the owner returns as a Muslim after joining, the mudarib remains on his mudaraba, unlike the agent. The difference is that the subject of disposal has left the ownership of the principal and the agent has no right to it, unlike the mudarib. The restriction 'by joining the owner' is because if the owner apostatizes but does not join, his actions are suspended. And the text indicates that if the mudarib apostatizes, the mudaraba remains as it was: if he buys, sells, profits, or loses, then dies apostate or joins the enemy, his transactions are valid, profit is shared as stipulated, and liability for all his transactions is on the owner according to Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage explains that a mudaraba contract terminates (tabtul) upon the death of either party, since it is a form of agency (wakalah) which is voided by death. Similarly, if the owner becomes an apostate and joins enemy territory (lahaq), it is treated like death. The commentary then explains why the mudarib is not treated exactly like a wakil (agent) in certain situations when recovering money from the owner: the mudarib can repeatedly recover payment from the owner if it perishes, while an agent can only recover once. This is because what the mudarib receives is a trust (amana), not a debt. Also, if the mudarib buys goods and sets them aside, he is not dismissed by that action, unlike an agent. If an apostate owner later returns as Muslim, the mudarib continues on his mudaraba, whereas an agent would be dismissed. The passage also notes that if the mudarib apostatizes, the mudaraba remains valid and his transactions are binding on the owner according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
لَحِقَ بِالمُرْتَدِّين (lahaqa bi'l-murtaddin): To join enemy territory as an apostate, which terminates the mudaraba like death.
أَمَانَة (amana): A trust; property held by someone without liability for loss unless negligent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The mudarib cannot recover again, the wakil can
B) The mudarib can recover again and again, the wakil cannot
C) Both can recover again and again
D) Neither can recover again

Key Takeaway
Mudaraba terminates upon death or apostasy of either party, and the mudarib's right to repeated recovery stems from the amana nature of his possession, distinguishing him from a wakil.

Loss in Mudaraba: Profit First, Capital Last
Ruling on loss coverage in three mudaraba scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios for loss coverage in mudaraba and apply the ruling to determine whether profit distribution must be revoked.

Core Idea
Loss in mudaraba is first absorbed by profit; the mudarib never guarantees capital loss.

Mudaraba is a partnership where the owner provides capital and the mudarib provides labor. A key question is who bears losses from market fluctuations. Unlike a loan, the mudarib is a trustee (amin) and not liable for capital loss unless negligent. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the sequence: losses are first covered by profits; only if profits are insufficient does the capital shrink. It also explains how profit distributions are adjusted when loss occurs later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The first sentence states the rule: 'Whatever is lost from the mudaraba is from the profit.' This means profits act as a buffer before capital is touched.
The mudarib does not owe compensation; he is a trustee.
2
Analyze the first scenario: loss during ongoing mudaraba
If loss exceeds profit, the mudarib is not liable. The capital reduces, but the mudarib's trust status remains.
This is the baseline: no personal guarantee.
3
Analyze the second scenario: loss after profit distribution, but the mudaraba continues
If they had distributed profit and later the capital (or part) perishes, the distribution is undone: the owner takes his full capital first, and any surplus is shared again. The mudarib does not cover the deficiency.
This ensures the owner's capital is restored before any profit is recognized.
4
Analyze the third scenario: loss after termination and new contract
If they distributed profit, terminated the mudaraba, then made a new mudaraba, and loss occurs, the first profit distribution is not revoked. The two contracts are independent.
The loss belongs to the new mudaraba.
5
Apply the rule in practice
To determine whether profit must be returned, check if the mudaraba continued or was terminated. Only if the same contract continues and capital is lost do they undo the previous distribution.
This is a conditional application: continuity matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما هلك من المضاربة فمن الربح فإن زاد الهالك على الربح لم يضمن المضارب وإن قسم الربح وبقيت المضاربة ثم هلك المال أو بعضه ترادا الربح ليأخذ المالك رأس ماله وما فضل فهو بينهما وإن نقص لم يضمن وإن قسم الربح وفسخت ثم عقداها فهلك المال لم يترادا الربح الأول.
Whatever is lost from the mudaraba is from the profit. If the loss exceeds the profit, the mudarib does not guarantee it. If they divided the profit, the mudaraba remained, then the capital or part of it perished, they return the profit so that the owner takes his capital, and what remains is between them. If there is a deficiency, the mudarib does not guarantee. If they divided the profit, terminated, then contracted again, and then the capital perished, they do not return the first profit.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage gives three scenarios for loss in mudaraba. First, during the contract, any loss is first deducted from profit. If loss exceeds profit, mudarib is not liable because he is an amin (trustee). Second, if they had already distributed profit, the mudaraba continues, and later some capital is lost, they undo the distribution: the owner takes his full capital first, and only then is any surplus shared. The mudarib does not compensate for the loss. Third, if they distributed profit, then terminated the contract, and later started a new mudaraba (without relation to the first), and then loss occurs, the first profit distribution stands and is not revoked.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudaraba): A partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, with profits shared according to an agreed ratio.
ربح (ribh): Profit generated from the mudaraba venture.
هلاك (halak): Loss or destruction of capital not due to mudarib's fault.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; the mudarib is not liable for market losses.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital contributed by the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if loss occurs after profit has been distributed but the mudaraba continues?
A) The profit distribution is final and loss comes from future profits only
B) They return the profit so the owner recovers his full capital first
C) The mudarib must compensate the loss
D) The contract becomes void

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi mudaraba, loss is first deducted from profit; the mudarib never guarantees capital loss, and if loss occurs after profit distribution under the same contract, the distribution is undone to protect the owner's capital.

Dismissal of the Mudarib and His Authority Over Goods
Ruling on dismissal of mudarib: effects if he knows and his authority over goods after dismissal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a mudarib's dismissal to take effect and apply the rules regarding his authority to sell goods after dismissal.

Core Idea
A mudarib is dismissed only upon knowledge; until then, his authority to sell goods remains intact.

In Mudarabah partnerships, the mudarib acts as an agent of the capital owner. Dismissal terminates this agency, but when does it actually take effect? This passage clarifies that knowledge of dismissal is essential. We explore the mudarib's authority over unsold goods after dismissal and the key distinction between actual and constructive dismissal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage discusses dismissal (azl) of the mudarib by the owner. Determine the condition for the dismissal to become effective: the mudarib must have knowledge.
Knowledge is defined as receiving a report from two men, or one just man if the mudarib is a fuduli.
2
Examine the effect on goods
If the mudarib knows of dismissal and the capital consists of goods (urud), he may sell them but cannot dispose of the sale proceeds. The owner cannot rescind that sale.
This is because the mudarib has a right to profit, which must be protected.
3
Distinguish between mudaraba and partnership
Note that in a regular partnership, dissolution by one partner is valid even regarding goods, but in mudaraba the mudarib retains authority until knowledge.
This highlights the unique nature of the mudarib's position.
4
Analyze constructive dismissal
Constructive dismissal (e.g., owner's death) also requires knowledge for the mudarib, unlike a regular agent who is dismissed immediately without knowledge.
The mudarib's right to profit explains this difference.
5
Apply the ruling to credit sales
After dismissal, the mudarib may sell goods for credit even if the owner forbade it, because prohibiting credit is akin to dismissal.
This broad authority stems from the mudarib's vested interest in profit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينعزل بعزله إن علم أي ينعزل المضارب بعزل رب المال إن علم به لأنه وكيل، وإن لم يعلم لا . والمراد بالعلم ما يستفاد من خبر رجلين مطلقاً أو واحد عدل إن كان فضولياً وإلا فخبر مميز قوله : ( وإن علم والمال عروض باعها ثم لا يتصرف في ثمنها ولا يملك المالك فسخها في هذه الحالة) لأن للمضارب حقاً في الربح. قيد بالمضاربة لأن أحد الشريكين إذا فسخ الشركة وما لها أمتعة قالوا يصح فسخه بخلاف المضاربة؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان من الشركة والمراد من العرض هنا أن يكون خلاف جنس رأس المال والدراهم والدنانير جنسان هنا، فإذا كان رأس المال دراهم وعزله ومعه دنانير له بيعها بالدراهم استحساناً، وله بيع العروض بعد العزل بالنقد والنسيئة وإن نهاه رب المال عن النسيئة كما يصح نهيه عن المسافرة في الروايات المشهورة، وكما لا يملك عزله لا يملك تخصيص الإذن لأنه عزل من وجه؛ كذا في النهاية. وشمل كلامه العزل الحكمي حتى لو كان له بيع العروض بعد موت رب المال حقيقة أو حكماً، ولا ينعزل في الحكمي إلا بالعلم بخلاف الوكيل حيث ينعزل في الحكمي وإن لم يعلم لأنه حق له بخلاف المضارب
And he is dismissed by his dismissal if he knows. That is, the mudarib is dismissed by the owner's dismissal if he knows of it, because he is an agent; if he does not know, he is not dismissed. Knowledge means what is derived from the report of two men unconditionally or one just man if he is a volunteer (fuduli), otherwise the report of a discerning person. And [the text says:] 'If he knows and the capital is goods, he sells them, then he does not dispose of their price, and the owner does not have the right to rescind that sale in this situation' because the mudarib has a right to profit. The restriction 'mudaraba' is because if one of two partners dissolves a partnership and they have goods, they say his dissolution is valid, unlike mudaraba. The meaning of 'goods' here is that they are of a different genus than the capital; dirhams and dinars are two genera here. So if the capital was dirhams and he sets them aside, and he has dinars, he may sell them for dirhams by istihsan. And he may sell the goods after dismissal for cash or credit even if the owner forbids credit, just as his prohibition of travel is valid in well-known transmissions. And just as he cannot dismiss him, he cannot restrict permission because it is a form of dismissal. This includes constructive dismissal (al-'azl al-hukmi), so that he may sell goods after the owner's death, actual or legal; but he is not dismissed in constructive dismissal unless he knows, unlike the agent who is dismissed in constructive dismissal even without knowledge because it is his own right, unlike the mudarib.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage discusses the dismissal (azl) of the mudarib. The mudarib is dismissed only if he knows of the dismissal. Knowledge means being informed by two reliable men or, if the mudarib is a fuduli (acting without permission in some matters), one just man suffices; otherwise, a discerning person's report. Once the mudarib knows of his dismissal but still has goods (urud) that are not yet sold, he may sell them because he has a right to profit, but he cannot use the sale price. The owner cannot rescind that sale. This differs from a partnership dissolution. The commentary explains the definition of 'goods' (urud) — they are anything other than dirhams and dinars. After dismissal, the mudarib may sell goods for cash or credit even if the owner forbade credit. Constructive dismissal (such as the owner's death) also allows selling goods, but the mudarib is not considered dismissed until he knows. This is unlike an agent who faces constructive dismissal even without knowledge.

Key Terms
عَزْل حُكْمِي (azl hukmi): Constructive dismissal; termination of agency due to an event like death, which may take effect without actual knowledge.
فُضُولِيّ (fuduli): A person who acts without prior authorization, e.g., a mudarib who exceeds his authority; here it refers to someone who is not a regular agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, he cannot sell
C) He can sell but only for cash
D) He can sell only if the owner permits

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's dismissal only becomes effective upon his knowledge, and his authority to sell goods continues until that point, protecting his right to profit.
